I wish I could point to a good general reference source for nanotechnology, but that is surprisingly difficult. K. Eric Drexler 
has done most to popularize the subject, albeit in a manner that owes as much to science fiction as to current scienceโsee his [3m
Engines of Creation [0m(London: Fourth Estate, 1990), and also K. E. Drexler, C. Peterson, and G. Pergamit (1994), [3mUnbounding the 
Future [0m(Reading, Mass.: Addison-Wesley, 1994). And, for critical comment on Drexlerโs vision of nanotechnology, see G. Stix, 
โTrends in Nanotechnology: Waiting for Breakthroughsโ, [3mScientific American [0m(April 1996), 94; and D. E. H. Jones, โTechnical 
Boundless Optimismโ, [3mNature, [0m374 (1995), 835. Ed Regisโs [3mNano! [0m(New York: Bantam Books, 1995), is a readable account, but centres 
around Drexlerโs vision. The volume by Crandall and Lewis (above) has some nice contributions, if slightly technical and now 
outdated. Michael Grossโs [3mTravels to the Nanoworld [0m(New York: Plenum, 1999), is well worth reading but sometimes idiosyncratic in 
its choices. The web site of Drexlerโs Foresight Institute (http://www.foresight.org) does a good job of collating up-to-date 
popular and technical articles. 

The work of Kinositaโs group is described in Y. Arai [3met al. [0m, โTying a Molecular Knot with Optical Tweezersโ, [3mNature, [0m399 (1999), 
446.

โArtificial Molecular Machinesโ, [3mAngewandte Chemie International Edition, [0m39 (2000), 3348โ91.
